        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="63311ebb853f4ff29cc44c09bc3f041a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '89a6f242-fc20-4660-89b0-a9dae9ceb5fe'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d82294721" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A facile but scalable strategy to decrease electrochemical polarization by accurately regulating multiple structural features for industrial-scale hard carbon enabling rapid sodium storage is proposed via adjusting the components of precursors.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7b2f99a5-d6e8-491a-88f6-884ff1a5efb9/anie202406889-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7b2f99a5-d6e8-491a-88f6-884ff1a5efb9/anie202406889-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/74379983-7278-403e-b342-34548feef000/anie202406889-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7b2f99a5-d6e8-491a-88f6-884ff1a5efb9/anie202406889-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d82294795" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Given the merits of abundant resource, low cost and high electrochemical activity, hard carbons have been regarded as one of the most commercializable anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, poor rate capability is one of the main obstacles that severely hinder its further development. In addition, the relationships between preparation method, material structure and electrochemical performance have not been clearly elaborated. Herein, a simple but effective strategy is proposed to accurately construct the multiple structural features in hard carbon via adjusting the components of precursors. Through detailed physical characterization of the hard carbons derived from different regulation steps, and further combined with <i>in-situ</i> Raman and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) analysis, the network of multiple relationships between preparation method, microstructure, sodium storage behavior and electrochemical performance have been successfully established. Simultaneously, exceptional rate capability about 108.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 8 A g<sup>−1</sup> have been achieved from RHC sample with high reversible capacity and desirable initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE). Additionally, the practical applications can be extended to cylindrical battery with excellent cycle behaviors. Such facile approach can provide guidance for large-scale production of high-performance hard carbons and provides the possibility of building practical SIBs with high energy density and durability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202406889-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202406889-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>SIBs are regarded as one of the most competitive candidates for grid-scale energy storage devices because of the economic advantages and excellent electrochemical performances.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0001_R_d82294642e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#anie202406889-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0003_R_d82294642e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun.">3</a></sup></span> However, the development of anode materials severely constrains their commercial applications.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0004_R_d82294642e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202406889-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0005_R_d82294642e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span> To date, large numbers of anode materials have been intensively studied for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0006_R_d82294642e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a>-<a href="#anie202406889-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0012_R_d82294642e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span> As the most successful commercial anode for lithium-ion battery, graphite shows poor Na storage behaviors due to the mismatch between the large ion radius of Na<sup>+</sup> and relatively narrow interlayer distance (&lt;0.37 nm).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0013_R_d82294642e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Mater. Today">13</a></sup></span> In contrast, hard carbon with complicated microstructure, composed of pores, defects, and graphitic microcrystallites, exhibits great potential for commercialization given its abundant resources, low cost and considerable capacity above 300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with low discharge voltage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0014_R_d82294642e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#anie202406889-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0015_R_d82294642e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Commun.">15</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, some severe issues are still remained and prevent their development of the industrialization process, especially undesirable specific capacity and ICE.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0016_R_d82294642e673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#anie202406889-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0017_R_d82294642e673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Environ. Sci.">17</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Currently, a variety of attempts have been made to overcome aforementioned obstacles.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0018_R_d82294642e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#anie202406889-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0019_R_d82294642e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Environ. Sci.">19</a></sup></span> For example, Titirici et al. synthesized various hard carbons using a serious of pretreatment strategies to rationally regulate microstructure in order to optimize specific capacity and ICE.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0020_R_d82294642e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Carbon Energy">20</a></sup></span> Wu and co-workers applied the surface engineering strategy and constructed hard carbons with high ICE about 81.1 %.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0021_R_d82294642e684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Carbon Energy">21</a></sup></span> In addition, hybrid-composite designing method was also adopted to tune interlayer distance and defective sites for carbon crystallites.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0022_R_d82294642e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Eng. J.">22</a></sup></span> Furthermore, metal ion catalyst approach has been developed in our group by employing Mn<sup>2+</sup> ions to accurately tailor graphitization degree, decrease defects, and remain effective Na<sup>+</sup> ions paths. A desirable specific capacity about 336 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and advanced ICE about 92.05 % can be achieved.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0023_R_d82294642e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Although the ICE and specific capacity are greatly enhanced, the rate capability performance is still not satisfactory for practical application. This may be ascribed to the reason that when fabricated as a full-cell device, the sodium precipitation phenomenon is so obvious that indicates the electrochemical polarization on the anode side is significant, hence, rate behaviors is in urgent need to be promoted.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0024_R_d82294642e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">24</a></sup></span> In this regard, a multi-porous carbon have been prepared via a co-pyrolysis method by Li et al. with desirable rate capability (208 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0025_R_d82294642e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">25</a></sup></span> In addition, Ding and co-workers synthesized the hard carbons with tuned interlayer distance and defect sites. The optimum hard carbon anode delivers excellent rate behavior about 205 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 6 C.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0026_R_d82294642e715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Electrochim. Acta">26</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, there are many issues should be considered for large-scale production from above mentioned strategies, the regulation of microstructure based on high price and complex procedures should be taken into account in practical application. Therefore, the biomass with low price is an excellent choice for large-scale production and some investigations about employing acid, alkali and water solution for washing treatment to enhance the electrochemical behaviors have emerged.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0027_R_d82294642e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Chem. A">27</a>-<a href="#anie202406889-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0032_R_d82294642e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ChemSusChem">32</a></sup></span> To date, there is no in-depth study about the improvement of rate performance through adjustment for the components of precursors. As such, it is of great significance to hunt for a simple process to realize the industrialization of hard carbons with desirable rate behaviors for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a facile but effective approach was developed and fulfilled high rate capability with desirable specific capacity and exceptional ICE of hard carbon in SIBs through adjusting the components of precursors. Meanwhile, such simple strategy have also been verified to enable kg-grade preparation of hard carbons. Furthermore, by systematically analyzing the microstructures and integrated with electrochemical characterizations, the network of multiple relationships between preparation method, microstructure, sodium storage behavior and electrochemical performance have been successfully established. More significantly, the electrochemical performances from half-cell, coin full-cell and cylindrical battery demonstrates that such easy and scalable method can offer possibilities of developing practical SIBs with high power density and durability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202406889-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202406889-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406889-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406889-sec-0003-title"> Structural Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The typical preparation process can be depicted in Figure S1 with acid treatment in different steps. Morphologies and microstructures of the hard carbons with different acid treatment process have been analyzed through scanning electron microscopy (SEM) (Figure S2a–d). Obviously, acid treatment process exhibits a significant effect on the morphology of the raw material, resulting in more fractional structures and smaller particle for RHC material. In specific, the influence of acid treatment on composition and structure of the bamboo powder can be illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0001">1a</a>. Generally speeking, the majority component of bamboo powder consists of cellulose, hemicellulose and lignin, and would lead to an acidolytic reaction with sulfide acid treatment. As such, structural deconstruction would occur and further some of the macromolecules would be degraded into some smaller molecules, such as glucose, monosaccharides and monocyclic aromatic compounds and so on.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0033_R_d82294642e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Environ. Sci.">33</a></sup></span> This can be confirmed from the loose structure after acid treatment for bamboo powder from Figure S3.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406889-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1c2f22b0-08b5-45a9-8e5f-159de2870843/anie202406889-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1c2f22b0-08b5-45a9-8e5f-159de2870843/anie202406889-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/205e7f22-92cf-4766-9d36-5aec70d3c5da/anie202406889-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1c2f22b0-08b5-45a9-8e5f-159de2870843/anie202406889-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202406889-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202406889" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) Schematic of the preparation process for hard carbon materials, (b) the FTIR spectra of untreated and acid-treated bamboo powder, (c) the Raman spectra of untreated and acid-treated bamboo powder, (d) the XRD pattern of untreated and acid-treated bamboo powder, (e) the percentage of different components in untreated and acid-treated bamboo powder, (f–i) high resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images of the UHC, RHC, PHC and SHC samples.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Meanwhile, Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0001">1b</a>) shows that some differences in peak transmittance in the acid-treated sample without new functional groups formation. A peak at 3340 cm<sup>−1</sup> ascribing to hydrogen bonded in cellulose fibers becomes weakened obviously, suggesting that acid treatment can break the hydrogen bond and result in structure change of bamboo powder.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0034_R_d82294642e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chin. J. Anal. Chem.">34</a></sup></span> The stretching characteristics of −CH<sub>3</sub> and −CH<sub>2</sub> can lead to the formation of the peak at 2924 cm<sup>−1</sup>. The transmittance of acid-treated sample is much weak, indicating the fracture of the carbon chain.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0035_R_d82294642e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Bioresour. Technol.">35</a></sup></span> Besides, the obviously change of the absorption at 1735 cm<sup>−1</sup> of the acid-treated sample represents the presence of broken bonds in the hemicelluloses.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0036_R_d82294642e788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Bioresour. Technol.">36</a></sup></span> Reduced intensity around 1513 cm<sup>−1</sup> standing for stretching and vibrations of the lignin aromatic ring can be ascribed to the removal of lignin to some extent. The reduced intensity of the band at 1245 cm<sup>−1</sup> assigned to the vibrations of C−O is derived from the cleavage of acetyl groups in the hemicellulose side chains.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0037_R_d82294642e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Green Chem.">37</a></sup></span> Additionally, other peaks at 1163, 1111 and 1035 cm<sup>−1</sup> attributing to C−O−C stretching vibration features are important characteristics of cellulose and become much weak in the acid-treated sample.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0038_R_d82294642e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Carbohydr. Polym.">38</a></sup></span> And the decreased characteristic peak intensity from Raman spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0001">1c</a>) of acid-treated sample confirms the microstructure change of bamboo powder after acid treatment.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0039_R_d82294642e807" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Environ. Manage.">39</a></sup></span> In addition, the improved degree of crystallinity for cellulose in the acid-treated sample (from 48.64 % to 50.94 %) based on the calculation from X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns demonstrates the dissolution of amorphous substances such as hemicellulose and lignin (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0001">1d</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0033_R_d82294642e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Environ. Sci.">33</a></sup></span> And the percentage of content for different components in Table S1 and Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0001">1e</a> shows that the increase of the cellulose and decrease of hemicellulose, lignin and impurities after acid treatment.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0040_R_d82294642e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Hazard. Mater.">40</a></sup></span> Hence, it can be speculated that the original structure could be damaged and form smaller particles after carbonization process. Besides, the bamboo powder only treated by distilled water also shows a little structural change (Figure S4), this may be ascribe to the penetration solution exhibits acidity with pH value about 5.0. Furthermore, it can be obviously seen that most of the K element in the impurities of the bamboo powder can be removed via acid treatment, and it would be different to be eliminated after pre-carbonized and second carbonized treatment (Figure S5). Therefore, it can be concluded that acid treatment can degrade organic long-chain macromolecules into small molecules, dissolve part of lignin and hemicellulose and remove some of the impurities, so that affect the original structure and would form a uniform pore structure during the pyrolysis process, which may result in suitable short-range graphite domain structure with more oxygen-enriched functional groups exposed on the surface and more reversible adsorption sites would generate.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0015_R_d82294642e823" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Commun.">15</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, the changes of microstructures from the as-prepared carbon samples have been observed in-depth via HRTEM. As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0001">1f</a>–<a href="#anie202406889-fig-0001">i</a>, HRTEM images from UHC, PHC and SHC samples exhibit long-range ordered lattice with uneven pore distributions, and thick carbon layers would result in relatively narrow carbon interlayer spacing, blocking the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into carbon layers. In contrast, RHC sample possesses short-range curved carbon layers with large open pore channels and is conducive to facilitate the transport and storage of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Meanwhile, more homogeneously porous structure in RHC sample can be apparently seen, which may be beneficial for the Na storage performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0041_R_d82294642e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Natl. Sci. Rev.">41</a></sup></span> Based on these observation, it demonstrates that the effect of the acid treatment is more conspicuous for raw material, while it show less influence on structures for the partially carbonized materials.</p>
            
            <p>The analysis from XRD measurement in Figure S6 also present consistent results with the HRTEM images. Compared with the broad XRD peak corresponding to (002) of RHC, there are obvious narrow peaks exhibited at about 26° for UHC, PHC and SHC, demonstrating the higher degree of graphitization for these three samples. Besides, to clearly distinguish the different crystal region in a broad (002) diffraction peak, the experimental XRD patterns were analyzed by profile fitting method (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0002">2a</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0042_R_d82294642e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a></sup></span> The area proportion of graphite-like regions of RHC sample is about 22.1 %, and increase to 28.8 % of PHC, 31.3 % of SHC and 36.6 % of UHC, which confirms the more disordered structure of RHC with the acid treatment for raw material (Table S2 and Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0002">2b</a>). Meanwhile, the area proportion of pseudo-graphitic regions of RHC sample achieves 35.936 % with the desirable Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion interlayer spacing of 0.372 nm. In addition, the area proportion of highly disordered regions of RHC sample is much higher (about 41.949 %) than the other three samples (25.282 % for UHC, 35.737 % for PHC, 33.242 % for SHC) with the interlayer spacing about 0.415 nm, which would facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> ions transportation and storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0043_R_d82294642e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Small">43</a></sup></span>
               </p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406889-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8e0814fd-04da-45f4-9e5b-522c8fcf2e13/anie202406889-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8e0814fd-04da-45f4-9e5b-522c8fcf2e13/anie202406889-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3e43bebe-dd72-46b9-bc8a-9d4da7ac3e00/anie202406889-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8e0814fd-04da-45f4-9e5b-522c8fcf2e13/anie202406889-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202406889-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202406889" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Structural characterization of the UHC, RHC, PHC and SHC samples: (a, b) XRD pattern and the area percentages of three different regions, (c, d) Raman pattern, A<sub>D3</sub>/A<sub>G</sub> and La values of the as-prepared samples, (e–g) the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherms, pore size distribution, specific surface area and pore volume.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">In order to further explain the change of the oxygen-containing defects and carbon structure of the as-prepared samples, Raman spectra were used (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0002">2c</a>). It can be fitted of D4, D1, D3, and G bands, which are calculated from area ratio of the peak (AD/AG).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0044_R_d82294642e897" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Chem. Mater.">44</a>-<a href="#anie202406889-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0046_R_d82294642e897" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nano Lett.">46</a></sup></span> The in-plane grain size <i>L</i>a can be calculated by the equation reported by Tuinstra-Koenig as follows:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202406889-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d34a5dd8-c918-43b6-88f4-b93d23dad288/anie202406889-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <p>It can be calculated and noted from Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0002">2d</a> and Table S3 that the RHC sample with acid treatment for raw material shows a higher AD1/AG about 1.97 (1.56, 1.72, 1.67 for UHC, PHC and SHC), a higher AD3/AG about 1.20 (0.78, 1.12, 1.15 for UHC, PHC and SHC), smaller <i>L</i>a values about 9.74 (12.32, 11.15, 11.54 for UHC, PHC and SHC). This result indicates that RHC sample possesses more oxygen-containing functional groups, more structural defects and shorter graphite domains, which will result in more reversible adsorption active sites compared to other samples, may attribute to the higher reversible capacity and higher ICE of RHC electrode.</p>
            
            <p>Comprehensive analyses of porous microstructure for the samples have been investigated and the typical IV type adsorption curves can be observed from the nitrogen adsorption/desorption isothermal curves (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0002">2e</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0047_R_d82294642e968" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Power Sources">47</a></sup></span> Specifically, the RHC sample reveals a higher specific surface area of 19.96 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and pore volume of 0.01185 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Table S4 and Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0002">2g</a>). Meanwhile, the pore size distribution curves (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0002">2f</a>) show that the pore size of RHC is mainly concentrated at around 3.81 nm, and exhibits more uniform pore structure than other samples, which are consistent with HRTEM images. The reason can be ascribed to that the acid treatment can effectively degrade the hemicellulose in bamboo powder and remove a small amount of lignin, which leads to the stripping of the cluster fibers from each other and increases the porosity and specific surface area of the biomass substrate.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0048_R_d82294642e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Microscopy: advances in scientific research and education.">48</a></sup></span> Higher specific surface area and more uniform porous microstructure are conducive to enhancing the contact area of electrode and electrolyte, providing more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, as well as offering more favorable ionic channel for Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion and the Na filling the pore.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0049_R_d82294642e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">49</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>The chemical composition and elemental status of the as-obtained materials have been investigated via X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurement. As depicted in Figure S7a that all the as-prepared materials are composed of C and O elements. Specifically, for the C1s fitting spectrum of RHC sample, four main peaks located at about 284.2 eV, 284.8 eV, 285.5 eV, and 286.4 eV can be ascribed to sp2-C, sp3-C, C−O, C=O functional groups.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0050_R_d82294642e999" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">50</a></sup></span> Additionally, as for the O1s fitting spectrum of RHC sample, two main peaks located at about 531.8 eV and 532.8 eV can be attributed to C−O and C=O (Table S4).<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0051_R_d82294642e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Mater. Chem. A">51</a></sup></span> Particularly, the percentage of C=O in RHC sample is higher than the other samples with 55.87 % (48.89 % for UHC, 54.88 % for PHC and 45.12 % for SHC), which is beneficial for the reversible adsorption of Na in the electrochemical process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0052_R_d82294642e1005" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">52</a></sup></span> Generally speaking, based on these fundamental aspects, the physical structure, including the degree of graphitization, interlayer spacing, specific surface area, and porous structure, the content of O-containing functional groups and configurations can be finely tuned and optimized by a sample acid treatment for the raw material.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406889-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406889-sec-0004-title"> Sodium Storage Performance of Half-Cell Device</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the electrochemical performance of the as-prepared hard carbons, galvanostatic discharge/charge measurements were performed in ether-based electrolyte at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0003">3a</a>). The RHC anode exhibited the highest reversible capacity (320.96 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.43 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) with a desirable ICE about 91.45 % (higher than the hard carbons from several published literatures in Table S5), compared to the UHC (246.54 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.34 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>), PHC (280.13 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.35 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) and SHC (253.29 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.33 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>). Even removing all impurities inside the raw materials, the promotion of specific capacities are very limited (Figure S8). Meanwhile, it can be obviously seen via the detailed analysis from Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0003">3b</a> that the reversible capacity &gt;0.1 V and &lt;0.1 V have been improved notably, especially for RHC sample by introduction sulfuric acid treatment before carbonization process. Particularly, the ICE from range &gt;0.1 V reached the maximum value about 88.47 %, demonstrating that such acid treatment for raw material is beneficial for providing more reversible adsorption active sites and result in higher specific capacity, which is agreement with the physical characterization results.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406889-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2495e874-1935-46ff-853f-b9675f2a73d5/anie202406889-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2495e874-1935-46ff-853f-b9675f2a73d5/anie202406889-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1ccbcca3-1e55-4674-92ca-b79ce4c8c81f/anie202406889-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2495e874-1935-46ff-853f-b9675f2a73d5/anie202406889-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202406889-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202406889" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) Comparison of the first charge/discharge curves, (b) the percentages of slope capacity and plateau capacity from the second discharge curve, (c) cycle behaviors of the UHC, RHC, PHC and SHC anodes, (d) electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) curves for the as-prepared four samples, (e) EIS curves for the UHC and RHC anode after 100 cycles, (f, g) HRTEM images of the SEI from UHC and RHC anodes, (h, i) XPS spectra of F 1s from SEI on UHC and RHC anodes.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To observe and deal with the differences of the charge/discharge curves for the prepared samples, the curve differential have been employed, which can amplify the change and easy to analyse. Specifically, the <i>d</i>Q/<i>d</i>V data (Figure S9) show that the electrodes exhibiting a pair of sharp redox peaks at about 0.06 V, which is consistent with the low-potential plateaus of hard carbons (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0003">3a</a>). Meanwhile, a strong redox peak of RHC sample can also be noted from the comparison of the curves, which can be attributed to the reason that the unique microstructure of RHC would enable more sodium storage sites and fast channels for sodium access, illustrating good reversibility of RHC sample.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0023_R_d82294642e1074" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements have been conducted to investigate the kinetics characteristic of the sodium storage process. The first two cycles of CV curves at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> in Figure S10 shows that the reversible redox peaks appear in the first cycle, demonstrating better solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer formation on the interface of RHC electrode/electrolyte.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0053_R_d82294642e1081" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Energy Storage Mater.">53</a>, <a href="#anie202406889-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0054_R_d82294642e1081" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Energy Mater.">54</a></sup></span> Besides, the CV curves of the prepared RHC is tested at 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup> and displays good reversibility features (Figure S11a). Meanwhile, CV measurements are applied and carried out to analyze the contributions about capacitive-controlled and diffusion-controlled process. The level of capacitive effect can be qualitative studied by plotting the ln (scan rate)-ln (peak current) curve based on Equation <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#anie202406889-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202406889-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/64ae4889-57f2-4a67-a4f6-b146ae71a496/anie202406889-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Here, <i>b</i> value represents a slope of the ln <i>i</i> versus ln <i>ν</i> curve. In specific, it indicates the diffusion-controlled process when <i>b</i> value approaches 0.5 and it suggests capacitive behavior when <i>b</i> value approaches 1.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0055_R_d82294642e1127" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Carbon">55</a>, <a href="#anie202406889-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0056_R_d82294642e1127" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nano Energy">56</a></sup></span> As can be noted that <i>b</i> values are more closer to 1, especially the <i>b</i> value for RHC are about 0.618 and −0.736, indicating that the kinetic behavior is dominated by capacitive-controlled process (Figure S11b). According to the previous studies, the proportion of capacitive capacity contribution can be further quantified:<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0057_R_d82294642e1134" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - J. Phys. Chem. C">57</a>-<a href="#anie202406889-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0060_R_d82294642e1134" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nat. Commun.">60</a></sup></span> the current response <i>i</i> at a specific potential <i>V</i> can be separated into surface capacitive behavior and diffusion-controlled processes:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202406889-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/de4af258-2302-416c-9596-887329a22157/anie202406889-math-0003.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <p>In Equation (3), the surface capacitive behavior can be described as <i>k<sub>1</sub>ν</i>, whereas the diffusion-controlled process can be expressed as <i>k<sub>2</sub>ν</i><sup><i>1/2</i></sup>. Thus, each fraction of contribution of the current at specific potential can be quantified through confirming <i>k<sub>1</sub></i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub></i>. Typical voltage profile of the capacitive current in comparison with the total current of RHC is displayed in Figure S11c. Consequently, capacitive-controlled process has achieved 59.30 % at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S11d), increasing scan rate leads to more capacitive process and the capacitive contribution increased to 91.54 % of the total capacity at 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The increase in capacitance contribution demonstrates that capacitive process is crucial for electrochemical storage especially at high current densities (Figure S12). Cycle stability is an essential factor for practical application of hard carbons, which are measured at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for the first cycles and under 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 300 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0003">3c</a>). Due to the unique microstructure of RHC anode and the activation process, the capacity of RHC undergo fluctuations in the initial stages and exhibits better cycle performance, with higher reversible capacity and higher capacity retention about 91.07 % compared to 90.03 %, 89.84 % and 87.12 % for UHC, PHC and SHC anodes.</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, the electrochemical behavior for Na storage process of the prepared hard carbons has been further analyzed through EIS measurement, especially for the interfacial charge transfer kinetics investigation. The Nyquist plots in Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0003">3d</a> exhibit a straight line and a semicircle for all curves, illustrating that the four samples possess the similar electrochemical process. Obviously, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> in RHC electrode are greatly lower (&lt;5 Ω) than other samples, verifying that acid treatment for raw material could promote interfacial charge transfer kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0061_R_d82294642e1221" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Adv. Mater.">61</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the straight line of RHC is more lean to Y-axis, indicating much faster diffusion process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0062_R_d82294642e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J. Power Sources">62</a></sup></span> Regarding the comparison of electrochemical behaviors of UHC and RHC, and to further confirm the stability of these two samples, the EIS measurement has also been conducted after 100 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0003">3e</a>) and shows that the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> derived from RHC anode are both lower than those in UHC anode, that is, 4.00 Ω versus 16.94 Ω for <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> and 35.69 Ω versus 76.29 Ω for <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, respectively. Besides, HRTEM has been used to analyze the microstructures of the SEI layer, the thickness of the SEI layer formed on the RHC surface (11.65 nm) is much thinner than that on the surface of UHC (41.01 nm) (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0003">3f</a>, <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0003">g</a>), and higher Na−F (66.90 %) concentration has been detected from the SEI of RHC anode (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0003">3h</a>, <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0003">i</a> and Figure S13), which would benefit the faster interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> transportation kinetics, and contribute to higher ICE and rate capability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0063_R_d82294642e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Mater.">63</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>Charge-storage kinetics of the hard carbon electrode is generally affected by polarization features during a typical electrochemical process, which can be effectively promoted by the structural regulation of the hard carbon material. Subsequently, it can be reflected by several electrochemical reaction process. Specifically, the rate performance under various current density was conducted to explore the charge-storage kinetics of all samples (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0004">4a</a>). The RHC sample presents best rate capability behaviors with a high capacity about 108.8 mA g<sup>−1</sup> even at 8.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0004">4b</a>–<a href="#anie202406889-fig-0004">c</a>, Figure S14 and Table S6). Moreover, the rate capabilities have also been measured in ester-based electrolyte and presented in Figure S15 and Figure S16. Obviously, the RHC anode shows a high specific capacity about 308.36 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with ICE about 86.54 % at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, displaying higher capacity than other three samples even under a high current density about 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Table S7). The results show much lower polarization of the as-prepared RHC anode. Further, the electrochemical behaviors of the RHC anode with high areal density about 3.6 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> have also been conducted (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0004">4d</a>–<a href="#anie202406889-fig-0004">f</a>). The specific capacity about 301.45 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and ICE about 90.9 % can be achieved and maintains about 230.93 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As widely known, sluggish kinetics is exhibited for SIBs when utilized below 0 °C with greatly influence through charge-transfer process at the electrode, electrode/electrolyte interface, as well as mass transfer process in the electrolyte.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0064_R_d82294642e1298" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">64</a>, <a href="#anie202406889-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0065_R_d82294642e1298" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Energy Mater.">65</a></sup></span> Here, electrochemical behavior of the RHC anode is also tested at −20 °C, the electrochemical behaviors presented in Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0004">4g</a>-i reveal a high capacity of 273.33 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and maintains about 211.90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high current density of 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which shows better sodium storage behavior than the UHC sample measured at room temperature. Furthermore, the cycle behaviors exhibit about 100 % capacity retention after 60 cycles with average Coulombic efficiency about 99.89 %. A thicker SEI can be observed from the RHC anode cycled at −20 °C and higher Na−F concentration can be probed (Figure S17). The significant achievements under high current density at room temperature, high areal density as well as low temperature confirms low polarization of the RHC anode and superior charge-storage ability.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406889-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c51e8423-28bb-481a-8d26-4beaebd09cc5/anie202406889-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c51e8423-28bb-481a-8d26-4beaebd09cc5/anie202406889-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/65e45efc-f8d2-40c2-9755-2df5055f5de3/anie202406889-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c51e8423-28bb-481a-8d26-4beaebd09cc5/anie202406889-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202406889-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202406889" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical behaviors of the half cells at various current densities: (a) rate performance of the UHC, RHC, PHC and SHC anodes, (b) the charge/discharge curves of the RHC anode at various current densities, (c) the specific capacity retention of the UHC, RHC, PHC and SHC anodes. Electrochemical behaviors of the RHC anode under high areal density: (d) first charge/discharge curve, (e) rate performance and (f) the charge/discharge curves at various current densities. Electrochemical behaviors of the RHC anode under low temperature: (g) rate performance, (h) charge/discharge curves at various current densities, and (i) cycle stability under about −20 °C.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406889-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406889-sec-0005-title"> Analysis of Sodium Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>In order to further comprehensive analysis the Na storage process, the first charge/discharge and <i>d</i>V/<i>d</i>Q data of different samples are conducted (Figure S18 and Table S8). Accordingly, the <i>d</i>V/<i>d</i>Q curves from the first and second discharge curves can be divided into two parts based on the slope for each curves, which represents the slope capacity and the plateau capacity. And the difference of the <i>d</i>V/<i>d</i>Q curves with red square frame compared to the second discharge curves can be ascribed to the formation of SEI and represent the loss of irreversible capacity in the slope capacity (in blue region). Additionally, from the comparison of the curves for RHC and UHC samples, it can be apparently observed that the capacities attributed to the slope and plateau region increase significantly, which can be corresponded to the optimized microstructure of the RHC sample, including more reversible O-containing functional groups, desirable degree of graphitization and interlayer spacing, higher specific surface area and more homogeneous pore structure.</p>
            
            <p>To systematically investigate the kinetic properties between different samples, GITT were performed during the initial discharge processes to evaluate the apparent diffusion coefficient (D<sub>a</sub>) of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0005">5a</a> presents four GITT curves and D<sub>a</sub> of Na<sup>+</sup> based on the calculation from Equation (S4). Notably, the similar GITT profiles indicate the similar sodium storage processes for these four hard carbons. Meanwhile, the diffusion coefficient increases first and than decreases at about 0.56 V, which can be ascribed to the formation of SEI layers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0023_R_d82294642e1372" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span> After that, because of the adsorption process for Na<sup>+</sup> of the electrodes and interlayer insertion processes, the diffusion coefficient appears to be a typical increase and fall process. And the diffusion coefficient increases in the range of 0.05 to 0 V, suggesting a typical pore filling process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0066_R_d82294642e1377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Carbon">66</a></sup></span> Furthermore, RHC exhibits incredible enhancement of D<sub>a</sub> value in the whole procedure compared to other samples, demonstrating that the acid treatment for bamboo powder is beneficial for construction of advanced porous structure, which can be held accountable for its unprecedented rate performance with fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0026_R_d82294642e1384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Electrochim. Acta">26</a></sup></span>
               </p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406889-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0fe19e78-e5ed-47c4-96c1-af731491dbe1/anie202406889-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0fe19e78-e5ed-47c4-96c1-af731491dbe1/anie202406889-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5e43b893-c5b7-4050-8315-e41637ebe7c0/anie202406889-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0fe19e78-e5ed-47c4-96c1-af731491dbe1/anie202406889-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202406889-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202406889" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) GITT curves of the UHC, RHC, PHC and SHC anodes, (b–e) <i>in-situ</i> Raman pattern and the related ID/IG value during the discharge process of the RHC anode, (f) schematic diagram of the sodium storage stages of RHC anode.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To further confirm the structural changes and Na storage mechanism of RHC sample, <i>in-situ</i> Raman (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0005">5b</a>–<a href="#anie202406889-fig-0005">d</a>) analysis have been utilized. It can be seen that D-band peak gradually become broaden and decreased from 2.20 to 0 V, but the position of this peak keeps unchanged, which can be attributed to the suppression of the breathing vibration of carbon rings at the defects and edges caused by the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> at defective sites and surface of pores.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0067_R_d82294642e1425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">67</a></sup></span> As for the G-band peak, the position remains unchanged from 2.20 to 0.10 V and gradually become red shift from 0.10 to 0 V in the discharging process and then gradually shifts back from 0 to 0.1 V in the discharging process, corresponding to the weakening and lengthening of the C−C bond due to the transfer of electrons to π* antibonding-band with the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the carbon layers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0015_R_d82294642e1431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Commun.">15</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the width of G-peak increased from 0.10 to 0 V, demonstrating the diffusion process of Na<sup>+</sup> to pore structures and interact with sp2 carbon atoms and resulting in the change of E<sub>2g</sub> stretching mode for the G-peak.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0068_R_d82294642e1438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Adv. Energy Mater.">68</a></sup></span> The aforementioned features strongly revealed the insertion of Na into the carbon layers and filling of carbon nanopores in the low potential region. In addition, the fitted curves from ID/IG values (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0005">5e</a>) show that the slope exhibits noticeable changes during the discharge process under the region of 1.0 to 0 V, and can be divided into five regions, which is in agreement with the variation trend of the GITT curves, indicating the changes in microstructure due to the Na storage procedures. Integrating the analysis from <i>d</i>V/<i>d</i>Q, GITT and <i>in-situ</i> Raman, the sodium storage mechanism can be described in the schematic diagram in Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0005">5f</a>, depicting three stages including adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> in active sites on surfaces or defects and interlayers, interlayer insertion dominant process, and pore filling dominant process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202406889-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202406889-bib-0069_R_d82294642e1456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69 - Adv. Energy Mater.">69</a></sup></span></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406889-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406889-sec-0006-title"> Electrochemical Characterizations of Coin Full-cell Device and 18650 Cylindrical Battery</h3>
            
            <p>Given the superior electrochemical behaviors of the as-prepared hard carbons, great potential for practical application has been exhibited. Herein, according to the yield of the carbons through different processing steps, the measured specific capacity is normalized to achieve a relative specific capacity and carry out the cost estimation (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0006">6a</a>, <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0006">b</a>). Obviously, although the yield of the RHC is lower than the other three samples, the relative specific capacity is still much higher. More importantly, the relative specific capacity retention of the RHC material are higher under various current densities. Furthermore, based on the cost estimation from low price of the cheap biomass precursor (about 278 USD/t for bamboo powder), solvent treatment (about 70 USD/t for H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>), and conventional carbonization process (primary and second carbonization, which may be similar to other hard carbon production process from other companies), it shows low-cost characteristic for large-scale production and application for SIBs.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202406889-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4adfeb73-1d3b-4838-8e06-9665dab751c2/anie202406889-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4adfeb73-1d3b-4838-8e06-9665dab751c2/anie202406889-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c7069492-6099-4d1b-aea2-ccd07b4d3ad8/anie202406889-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4adfeb73-1d3b-4838-8e06-9665dab751c2/anie202406889-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202406889-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202406889" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) Relative specific capacity calculated based on the specific capacity and yields, (b) relative specific capacity retention at different current densities of the UHC, RHC, PHC and SHC anodes, (c) the working principle illustration of the full-cell. Electrochemical behaviors of the PB//RHC coin cell: (d) charge/discharge curve, (e) rate performance and (f) cycle behavior at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Electrochemical behaviors of the 18650 cylindrical battery: (g) charge/discharge curve, (h) rate performance and (i) cycle behavior at 0.5 C.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To further evaluate the practical performance, the coin full-cells with RHC anode and Prussian blue (PB) cathode (charge/discharge curve in Figure S19) have been fabricated and the physical characterization of commercial PB is presented in Figure S20, the working mechanism of full-cell is illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0006">6c</a>. Impressively, the coin full-cell device exhibits high capacity about 268.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on anode) with energy densities of 278.5 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on both active substance mass of cathode and anode) at 0.1 C (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0006">6d</a>) and exhibit good rate capability with high capacity of 164.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0006">6e</a>). Moreover, the cycling behavior shows that ~70.74 % capacity retention at 1.0 C after 500 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0006">6f</a> and Figure S21a) with average CE about 99.81 % (Figure S21b), suggesting the great potential of the practical hard carbon anodes for high-power SIBs.</p>
            
            <p>To assess the feasibility of practical application of RHC material, a scale-up preparation process is conducted, and Figure S22 shows the acid treatment process and the final product after high carbonization process, which is based on the expansion of RHC preparation process. Significantly, the cylindrical batteries are fabricated by employing the scalable prepared RHCs as anode and commercial PB as cathode counterpart. The fabrication process and the computed tomography picture of the 18650 cylindrical battery have been presented in Figure S23–24. The corresponding charge/discharge curve at 0.1 C present obvious discharge plateaus around 3.1 V with reversible capacity about 722.5 mAh (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0006">6g</a>). Besides, good rate capability has been exhibited with capacity retention about 88.8 % at 2 C (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0006">6h</a>). Long cycle stability over 200 cycles has been measured with voltage range between 1.6 and 3.6 V. In addition, the capacity retention is remained about 80.89 % over 200 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202406889-fig-0006">6i</a> and Figure S25a) with average CE about 99.31 % (Figure S25b). More importantly, it can be seen that the anode of the unwrapped 18650 battery shows no sodium precipitation phenomenon (Figure S26), indicating the great potential for practical applications of this fabricated devices.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202406889-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202406889-sec-0007-title"> Other precursor for RHCs</h3>
            
            <p>The above results suggest that such acid treatment approach can be applied to efficiently develop hard carbons with desirable microstructures for high-performance sodium storage by employing bamboo powder serving as the precursor. To exhibit the general method by applying acid treatment approach to construct microstructures of hard carbons, cellulose and gourd are selected as precursors with the same preparation process to investigate Na storage behaviors. As expected, the RHC-Cellulose and RHC-Gourd (Figure S27 and Figure S28) can realize greater improvement of reversible capacity (318.53 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with ICE about 93.36 % for RHC-Cellulose and 309.88 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with ICE about 91.25 % for RHC-Gourd) and rate capability behaviors (179.83 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 8 A g<sup>−1</sup> for RHC-Cellulose and 82.44 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 8 A g<sup>−1</sup> for RHC-Gourd). The results based on the acid-treated cellulose suggest that acid treatment to remove the impurities from biomass precursors and result in porous structure is not the main reason for the improvement of Na storage behavior. More significantly, it also confirms that introducing acid treatment for raw material can be an effective approach to accurately construct multiple structural features for hard carbons and enhance sodium storage behaviors, especially the rate capability.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202406889-sec-0008">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202406889-sec-0008-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, hard carbons with exceptional high rate performance have been developed via a simple but effective strategy by introducing sulfuric acid treatment to adjust the components of precursors. Comprehensive structural characterization and detailed investigation of sodium storage mechanism in this report provide clear insights into the relationships between preparation methods, microstructure of hard carbons and the corresponding capacity performance and sodium storage behavior. In specific, the as-prepared RHC exhibits advanced electrochemical behaviors in terms of high reversible capacity (320.96 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), desirable ICE (91.45 %) as well as excellent rate capability (108.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 8 A g<sup>−1</sup>). Such easy approach can also be verified on other precursors and promoted rate behaviors can also be achieved. Additionally, when fabricated with <a href="https://www.x-mol.com/paper/1527016755909636096?adv">PB cathode,</a> the coin full-cell delivers excellent cycle performance, exceptional rate capability as well as high energy density about 278.5 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. More significantly, the practical applications can be extended to cylindrical battery devices with outdtanding Na storage behaviors. We believe that this work could pave the way for large-scale production of hard carbons and constructing advanced SIBs in the future.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
